reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Dilated convolution tower with exponentially increasing dilation rates and residual connections.
    Each block applies LayerNorm -> GELU -> DilatedConv1d, then adds back the original input as a residual.
    This architecture is a core building block of genomic foundation models (Enformer, Basenji, Caduceus)
    for capturing multi-scale sequence dependencies in DNA/RNA.
    """
    def __init__(self, channels, seq_len, kernel_size, max_dilation_power):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.norms = nn.ModuleList()
        self.convs = nn.ModuleList()
        self.activation = nn.GELU()
        for i in range(max_dilation_power + 1):
            dilation = 2 ** i
            self.norms.append(nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len]))
            self.convs.append(nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation))

    def forward(self, x):
        residual = x
        for norm, conv in zip(self.norms, self.convs):
            x = norm(x)
            x = self.activation(x)
            x = conv(x)
            x = x + residual
        return x

batch_size = 32
channels = 256
seq_len = 4096
kernel_size = 3
max_dilation_power = 8

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [channels, seq_len, kernel_size, max_dilation_power]

We need to replace PyTorch model (dilated conv tower) with custom CUDA kernel. Let's first understand the model. The model: - Batch size B=32 - Channels C=256 - Sequence length L=4096 - Kernel size K=3 - max_dilation_power=8 => there are 9 blocks (i=0..8). Dilation = 2^i. - Each block: LayerNorm over channels and seq_len? Wait, nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len]) normalizes over the last two dimensions (channels, seq_len). In input shape (B, C, L), LayerNorm computes mean and variance over C and L, per batch element. So each sample is normalized across all positions and channels. Then GELU applied elementwise. Then Conv1d with kernel_size=K, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation. Input is (B, C, L). Convolution with groups=1 (default). Output shape: Since padding=dilation, output length = floor((L + 2*dilation - dilation*(K-1) - 1)/1) + 1. Let's compute: L + 2d - d*(K-1) -1 +1 = L + 2d - d*(K-1) = L + d*(2 - (K-1)). For K=3, K-1=2, so output length = L + d*(2-2) = L. So output length stays L. Good. - Then residual addition: x = x + residual, where residual is the original input before the block. So each block adds the input to the block back. Wait, residual is set to x before the loop, and not updated inside loop. Actually in the code: ``` residual = x for norm, conv in zip(self.norms, self.convs): x = norm(x) x = self.activation(x) x = conv(x) x = x + residual return x ``` So `residual` is set once before the loop. Thus each block receives the output of the previous block as input, but adds the original `x` (pre-loop) to the conv output. That's a bit unusual: all blocks share the same residual (the initial input). Actually check: after first iteration, x becomes conv(x) + residual. In second iteration, x = norm(x) where x is conv output + residual. Then activation, conv, + residual (original). So yes, each block adds the original input. We need to implement this as a single fused CUDA kernel, or multiple kernels but fused as much as possible. Since there are 9 blocks, each with LayerNorm, GELU, Conv1d, and residual add. We can try to fuse the entire sequence into one kernel. But given the dependencies and shared residual, it's possible to compute each block sequentially. However, we can at least fuse the operations within each block. Since there are 9 blocks, maybe a kernel per block is okay if well optimized. But we can also try to keep weights in registers or shared memory. Let's think about the dimensions. B=32, C=256, L=4096. Total elements per tensor: 32*256*4096 = 33,554,432 ~ 33.5M. In fp32, that's 134 MB per tensor. 9 blocks -> a lot of memory if not fused. Fusing each block reduces memory traffic significantly. The reference in PyTorch: LayerNorm (reads x, writes x), GELU (reads x, writes x), Conv1d (reads x, reads weights, writes output), add (reads output and residual, writes output). So per block, memory traffic: read x (for LN), write x (LN out), read x (GELU), write x (GELU out), read x (conv), read weight+bias, write conv out, read conv out and residual, write final x. That's many passes. Fusing block into one kernel: read x and residual, compute LN, GELU, conv, add residual, write output. That would be ideal. But conv1d is a locality-sensitive operation. Since K=3 and dilations vary up to 2^8=256, the effective kernel span is (K-1)*dilation = 2*256 = 512. That's large but manageable. L=4096. For each channel, we need to gather 3 inputs spaced by dilation. If we process each channel independently, we can load the input tile into shared memory? Since dilation can be up to 256, a tile of length say 256 would need to load extra halo of 512 on each side? That's too large. Alternatively, we can process the entire sequence per channel per block using direct global memory loads. Since K=3, each output element reads 3 input values. So memory reads per output = 3 * C (for all input channels). But conv1d is C_in=C_out=256, so each output channel is a dot product over C_in * K = 256*3 = 768 floats. That's 768 reads from input and 768 reads from weight (but weights can be cached). The bias is 1 read. Arithmetic intensity: each output element requires 768 multiply-adds + 768 adds for bias? If we process one output channel at a time, we need to read 768 input floats per output element. The weights (768 floats) can be kept in registers/shared memory if we process all output channels or a group. But reading 768 input floats per output element from global memory is heavy. However, there is reuse across output channels: same input positions are used for all output channels. So if we process a tile of output channels together, we can load input floats once and reuse them for all output channels. That's typical. But note that LayerNorm is across all channels and positions. So before conv, we need to compute mean and variance of the entire (C, L) tensor per batch element. Computing LN requires reading all elements of x (per batch). Then we need to normalize each element. Then GELU. Then conv. So fusing LN+GELU+Conv is tricky because LN needs global reduction per batch. We can do a two-pass approach: first compute mean and variance per batch (reduction kernel), then a second kernel that does normalize+GELU+conv+residual. Or we can fuse everything into one kernel per block with two passes: one thread block per batch element. Compute mean/variance in shared memory, then compute conv output in the same kernel. Actually, __syncthreads() is a barrier: all threads in the block must execute it before any can proceed. So the warps with divergent threads will wait for the accumulating threads to finish. Then they synchronize. That's fine. So the structure is safe. Now, reducing mean and variance. We need a reduction kernel. Since B=32, we can have one block per batch element. Each block reduces C*L = 1,048,576 elements. Block size: 1024 threads. Each thread handles ~1024 elements. Algorithm: ``` __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd(const float* x, float* mean, float* rstd, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < N; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = x[b * N + idx]; sum += val; sum_sq += val * val; } // block reduce __shared__ float ssum[1024]; __shared__ float ssq[1024]; ssum[tid] = sum; ssq[tid] = sum_sq; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { ssum[tid] += ssum[tid + s]; ssq[tid] += ssq[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { float m = ssum[0] / N; float m2 = ssq[0] / N; float var = m2 - m * m; mean[b] = m; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } ``` This is straightforward. Now, the fused conv kernel. We need to handle arbitrary block sizes (grid dims). Let's write the conv kernel with template or runtime parameters for L_PER_BLOCK and C_OUT_PER_BLOCK? Since we know the sizes at compile time, we can hardcode them for efficiency. The parameters B, C, L are fixed in the problem statement, but the evaluation may call with different values? Usually the evaluation uses the same sizes. The constructor arguments are fixed: channels=256, seq_len=4096, kernel_size=3, max_dilation_power=8. So we can safely assume these values. But to be generic, we can pass them as arguments. However, for performance, we want compile-time constants for shared memory sizes, but we can also use dynamic shared memory. Using static shared memory with #define is easiest if dimensions are fixed. Let's use fixed L_PER_BLOCK=64, C_OUT_PER_BLOCK=8. But note: if L is not divisible by 64 (it is 4096 = 64*64, so fine). C=256 = 8*32, fine. So no boundary issues for the main tiles, but we should still include checks for safety. Now, writing the CUDA kernel in C++. We need to define: - `reduce_mean_rstd` - `fused_block_forward` The `fused_block_forward` signature: ``` void fused_block_forward(const float* x, const float* residual, const float* weight, const float* bias, const float* mean, const float* rstd, float* out, int B, int C, int L, int dilation) ``` Inside the kernel: ``` const int L_PER_BLOCK = 64; const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; const int K = 3; int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out_tile = blockIdx.y; int l_tile = blockIdx.z; int l_start = l_tile * L_PER_BLOCK; int c_out_start = c_out_tile * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int l_local = threadIdx.x; // 0..L_PER_BLOCK-1 int c_local = threadIdx.y; // 0..C_OUT_PER_BLOCK-1 int tid = c_local * L_PER_BLOCK + l_local; int global_l = l_start + l_local; int global_c_out = c_out_start + c_local; // load weights into smem __shared__ float w_sm[256][3][C_OUT_PER_BLOCK]; int w_total = C * K * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; for (int idx = tid; idx < w_total; idx += L_PER_BLOCK * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK) { int c_l = idx % C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tmp = idx / C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int k = tmp % K; int c_in = tmp / K; int c_out = c_out_start + c_l; if (c_out < C) { w_sm[c_in][k][c_l] = weight[((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + k]; } else { w_sm[c_in][k][c_l] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); float acc = 0.0f; int smem_len = L_PER_BLOCK + 2 * dilation; __shared__ float in_sm[L_PER_BLOCK + 2 * 256]; // max 576 float m = mean[b]; float rs = rstd[b]; int x_offset_base = b * C * L; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { // load input tile for (int p = tid; p < smem_len; p += L_PER_BLOCK * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK) { int pos = l_start - dilation + p; float val = 0.0f; if (pos >= 0 && pos < L) { val = x[x_offset_base + c_in * L + pos]; val = (val - m) * rs; // exact gelu float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); val = val * cdf; } in_sm[p] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (global_l < L && global_c_out < C) { float v0 = in_sm[l_local + 2 * dilation]; float v1 = in_sm[l_local + dilation]; float v2 = in_sm[l_local]; acc += w_sm[c_in][0][c_local] * v0 + w_sm[c_in][1][c_local] * v1 + w_sm[c_in][2][c_local] * v2; } __syncthreads(); } if (global_l < L && global_c_out < C) { acc += bias[global_c_out]; acc += residual[x_offset_base + global_c_out * L + global_l]; out[x_offset_base + global_c_out * L + global_l] = acc; } ``` Wait, we used `L_PER_BLOCK * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK` as block size = 64*8=512. For 1024 threads, we can do warp shuffle: ``` float sum = 0, sq = 0; for (int idx = tid; idx < N; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = x[b*N + idx]; sum += val; sq += val*val; } // warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, offset); } __shared__ float ssum[32]; __shared__ float ssq[32]; if (lane == 0) { ssum[warp_id] = sum; ssq[warp_id] = sq; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float sum2 = (lane < num_warps) ? ssum[lane] : 0; float sq2 = (lane < num_warps) ? ssq[lane] : 0; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum2 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum2, offset); sq2 += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq2, offset); } if (lane == 0) { mean[b] = sum2 / N; float m2 = sq2 / N; float var = m2 - mean[b]*mean[b]; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } ``` This uses warp shuffle and is fast. 1024 threads = 32 warps. First, the reduction kernel: ```cpp __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ mean, float* __restrict__ rstd, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; const float* xb = x + b * N; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; // Grid-stride loop with float4 vectorized loads int idx = tid; int block_size = blockDim.x; // Ensure N is divisible by 4 int N4 = N / 4; const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(xb); for (int i = idx; i < N4; i += block_size) { float4 v = x4[i]; sum += v.x + v.y + v.z + v.w; sum_sq += v.x * v.x + v.y * v.y + v.z * v.z + v.w * v.w; } // Tail int remainder_start = N4 * 4; for (int i = remainder_start + idx; i < N; i += block_size) { float v = xb[i]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } // Warp shuffle reduction for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float ssum[32]; __shared__ float ssq[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; if (lane == 0) { ssum[warp_id] = sum; ssq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < num_warps) { float ws = ssum[lane]; float wsq = ssq[lane]; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { ws += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, ws, offset); wsq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wsq, offset); } if (lane == 0) { float m = ws / N; float m2 = wsq / N; float var = m2 - m * m; mean[b] = m; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } } ``` This kernel assumes blockDim.x is a multiple of 32 and <=1024. We'll launch with 1024 threads per block. N=1,048,576. N/4 = 262,144. block_size=1024. So each thread does 256 iterations of float4 loop. That's fine. But we only have B blocks. B=32. That's fine. Now, the conv kernel: ```cpp __global__ void fused_block_forward_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ residual, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mean, const float* __restrict__ rstd, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int L, int K, int dilation) { // Grid: (B, C_out_tiles, 1) or (B, grid_c, 1) // Block: (THREADS) 1D const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; const int THREADS = 256; int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out_tile = blockIdx.y; int c_out_start = c_out_tile * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Dynamic shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; int smem_len = L + 2 * dilation; float* in_sm = smem; float* w_sm = smem + smem_len; // Load weights for this tile into shared memory int w_total = C * K * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; for (int idx = tid; idx < w_total; idx += THREADS) { int c_local = idx % C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tmp = idx / C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int k = tmp % K; int c_in = tmp / K; int c_out = c_out_start + c_local; if (c_out < C) { w_sm[idx] = weight[((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + k]; } else { w_sm[idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); float m = mean[b]; float rs = rstd[b]; // We will process 16 l positions per thread (since L=4096, THREADS=256) // For generality, compute positions per thread int l_per_thread = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; int l_start = tid * l_per_thread; int l_end = min(l_start + l_per_thread, L); // Local accumulators for each c_out in tile float acc[C_OUT_PER_BLOCK]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) acc[c] = 0.0f; int x_base = b * C * L; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { // Load padded input into smem for (int p = tid; p < smem_len; p += THREADS) { int pos = p - dilation; // maps p=0 -> pos=-d, p=smem_len-1 -> pos=L-1+d float val = 0.0f; if (pos >= 0 && pos < L) { val = x[x_base + c_in * L + pos]; val = (val - m) * rs; // exact GELU float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); val = val * cdf; } in_sm[p] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Accumulate for this thread's l range for (int l = l_start; l < l_end; ++l) { float v0 = in_sm[l + 2 * dilation]; float v1 = in_sm[l + dilation]; float v2 = in_sm[l]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int widx0 = (c_in * K + 0) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; int widx1 = (c_in * K + 1) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; int widx2 = (c_in * K + 2) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; acc[c] += w_sm[widx0] * v0 + w_sm[widx1] * v1 + w_sm[widx2] * v2; } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output for (int l = l_start; l < l_end; ++l) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int c_out = c_out_start + c; if (c_out < C) { float val = acc[c] + bias[c_out] + residual[x_base + c_out * L + l]; out[x_base + c_out * L + l] = val; } } } } ``` Wait, weight shared memory indexing: I stored weights linearly in `w_sm` with the same mapping as the global weight but transposed. Actually, `w_sm[idx]` where `idx` corresponds to `(c_in, k, c_local)`. The global weight index is `((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + k`. So `w_sm[idx] = weight[...]`. For reading, `widx = (c_in * K + k) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c`. That's correct. But note: in the accumulation loop, we are iterating `c_in` from 0 to C-1. For each `c_in`, we compute `widx` based on `c_in`. This means we are reading `w_sm` sequentially by `c_in`. Since all threads in the block have the same `c_in` at a given time, and they read different `c` values, the access pattern is: for fixed `c_in,k`, threads read `w_sm[base + c]` for c=0..7. Since they are consecutive in memory, bank conflicts? `w_sm` is a 1D array. `base` = `(c_in * K + k) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK`. Threads reading consecutive addresses (base + c) will have strided access? Actually, each thread may be reading a different `c`. But since threads in a warp execute the inner loop in lockstep? The inner loop `#pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c)`. In a warp, all threads execute the same `c` iteration simultaneously. So in the first iteration (c=0), all threads read `w_sm[widx0]` where `widx0` differs only by `c`? Wait, `widx0 = (c_in*K+0)*C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c`. If all threads have the same `c_in` and `c`, then all threads read the SAME address (since `c` is the same for all threads in that iteration). That is a broadcast! No bank conflict. Then they multiply by different `v0` (since each thread handles different `l`). Wait, actually each thread handles a range of `l`. In the loop `for (int l = l_start; l < l_end; ++l)`, all threads in a warp may have different `l_start` and `l_end`? If `l_per_thread` is constant (16), then threads in a warp have consecutive `tid`. `l_start = tid * 16`. So warp 0: tid 0..31 -> l_start 0, 16, 32, ..., 496. Actually, we can allocate `float acc[16][8];` to support L up to 16384 with 1024 threads. That's 128 floats. With 1024 threads, that's 131,072 registers per block > 65,536. So it will spill. But for L=4096, we only need 32 floats. If we declare `float acc[16][8];` and the compiler sees that only a runtime variable `l_per_thread` is used up to 4, will it optimize away the extra registers? Usually, if the index is not a compile-time constant, the compiler must allocate the whole array in registers or local memory. It might allocate in local memory for large arrays. For 128 floats, it will likely spill to local memory. That's not good. So better to make `L_PER_THREAD` a template parameter. In the wrapper, we can compute `L_PER_THREAD = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS`. Since L is known at runtime, we can use a switch: ``` void launch_kernel(...) { int lpt = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; if (lpt <= 2) { fused_block_forward_kernel<2><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(...); } else if (lpt <= 4) { fused_block_forward_kernel<4><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(...); } else if (lpt <= 8) { fused_block_forward_kernel<8><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(...); } else { fused_block_forward_kernel<16><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(...); } } ``` And the kernel is templated on `MAX_L_PER_THREAD`: ``` template <int MAX_LPT> __global__ void fused_block_forward_kernel(...) { int lpt = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; float acc[MAX_LPT][C_OUT_PER_BLOCK]; // use only first lpt entries } ``` This way, for L=4096, lpt=4, we instantiate with MAX_LPT=4, and the compiler knows the array size is 4*8=32. Perfect. If L is unknown, we can have up to MAX_LPT=16. We can have a few template instantiations. This is standard. Given the problem parameters, we can just hardcode `MAX_LPT=4` and assert L==4096. But to be robust, template is better. Alternatively, we can set `THREADS = L` if L <= 1024? But L=4096. Let's settle on templating with a few values: 1,2,4,8,16. Now, weight indexing in smem. Since w_sm is relatively small, we can load into a multi-dimensional array: `float w_sm[C][K][C_OUT_PER_BLOCK];` where C is runtime (256) but the compiler might not like variable-length arrays in shared memory? Actually, dynamic shared memory is 1D. We can index manually: ``` float* w_sm = smem + smem_len; #define WSM(c_in, k, c_local) w_sm[((c_in)*K + (k))*C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + (c_local)] ``` This works. Now, the conv kernel: ```cpp template <int MAX_LPT> __global__ void fused_block_forward_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ residual, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mean, const float* __restrict__ rstd, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int L, int K, int dilation) { const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; const int THREADS = 1024; int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out_tile = blockIdx.y; int c_out_start = c_out_tile * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lpt = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; if (lpt > MAX_LPT) return; // safety extern __shared__ float smem[]; int smem_len = L + 2 * dilation; float* in_sm = smem; float* w_sm = smem + smem_len; // load weights int w_total = C * K * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; for (int idx = tid; idx < w_total; idx += THREADS) { int c_local = idx % C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tmp = idx / C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int k = tmp % K; int c_in = tmp / K; int c_out = c_out_start + c_local; float wval = 0.0f; if (c_out < C) { wval = weight[((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + k]; } w_sm[idx] = wval; } __syncthreads(); float m = mean[b]; float rs = rstd[b]; int x_base = b * C * L; // accumulators float acc[MAX_LPT][C_OUT_PER_BLOCK]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < MAX_LPT; ++i) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { acc[i][c] = 0.0f; } } for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { // load padded input for (int p = tid; p < smem_len; p += THREADS) { int pos = p - dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (pos >= 0 && pos < L) { val = x[x_base + c_in * L + pos]; val = (val - m) * rs; float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); val = val * cdf; } in_sm[p] = val; } __syncthreads(); // accumulate for (int i = 0; i < lpt; ++i) { int l = tid + i * THREADS; if (l >= L) break; float v0 = in_sm[l + 2 * dilation]; float v1 = in_sm[l + dilation]; float v2 = in_sm[l]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int widx0 = (c_in * K + 0) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; int widx1 = (c_in * K + 1) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; int widx2 = (c_in * K + 2) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; acc[i][c] += w_sm[widx0] * v0 + w_sm[widx1] * v1 + w_sm[widx2] * v2; } } __syncthreads(); } // write output for (int i = 0; i < lpt; ++i) { int l = tid + i * THREADS; if (l >= L) break; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int c_out = c_out_start + c; if (c_out < C) { float val = acc[i][c] + bias[c_out] + residual[x_base + c_out * L + l]; out[x_base + c_out * L + l] = val; } } } } ``` Wait, `MAX_LPT` must be a compile-time constant. The `#pragma unroll` loops over `MAX_LPT` will unroll fully. Good. Now, the wrapper function will compute `lpt = (L + 1023) / 1024`. If `lpt` is say 4, we call the template with `<4>`. We also need to define the reduction kernel. Let's define a generic one with warp shuffle. ```cpp __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ mean, float* __restrict__ rstd, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; const float* xb = x + b * N; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; // vectorized loads int N4 = N / 4; const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(xb); for (int i = tid; i < N4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 v = x4[i]; sum += v.x + v.y + v.z + v.w; sum_sq += v.x * v.x + v.y * v.y + v.z * v.z + v.w * v.w; } for (int i = N4 * 4 + tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float v = xb[i]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } // warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float ssum[32]; __shared__ float ssq[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; if (lane == 0) { ssum[warp_id] = sum; ssq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float ws = (lane < num_warps) ? ssum[lane] : 0.0f; float wsq = (lane < num_warps) ? ssq[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { ws += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, ws, offset); wsq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wsq, offset); } if (lane == 0) { float m = ws / N; float m2 = wsq / N; float var = m2 - m * m; mean[b] = m; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } } ``` This kernel uses `blockDim.x` threads. We can launch with 1024 threads per block. But only B blocks. B=32. That's okay. But wait: `blockDim.x` is used in the loop. If blockDim.x = 1024, then `i += 1024`. N4 = 262,144. Threads per block = 1024. Each thread does 256 iterations of float4. That's 1024 loads per thread. With 1024 threads, total loads = 1,048,576 float4s = exactly the data. Good. The tail loop: `N4*4 + tid` = 1,048,576 + tid. Since N=1,048,576, `N4*4 = N`, so the tail loop doesn't execute. Good. Now, the wrapper functions: ```cpp void fused_block_forward(const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& residual, const torch::Tensor& weight, const torch::Tensor& bias, const torch::Tensor& mean, const torch::Tensor& rstd, torch::Tensor& out, int dilation) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); int K = weight.size(2); // assume (C, C, K) const int THREADS = 1024; const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; int lpt = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; dim3 grid(B, (C + C_OUT_PER_BLOCK - 1) / C_OUT_PER_BLOCK); dim3 block(THREADS); int smem_len = L + 2 * dilation; int w_size = C * K * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; size_t shared_mem = (smem_len + w_size) * sizeof(float); // Set max dynamic shared memory if needed if (shared_mem > 48 * 1024) { cudaFuncSetAttribute(fused_block_forward_kernel<16>, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, shared_mem); } // Cast to raw pointers const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); const float* res_ptr = residual.data_ptr<float>(); const float* w_ptr = weight.data_ptr<float>(); const float* b_ptr = bias.data_ptr<float>(); const float* m_ptr = mean.data_ptr<float>(); const float* r_ptr = rstd.data_ptr<float>(); float* o_ptr = out.data_ptr<float>(); switch (lpt) { case 1: fused_block_forward_kernel<1><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); break; case 2: fused_block_forward_kernel<2><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); break; case 3: fused_block_forward_kernel<4><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); break; case 4: fused_block_forward_kernel<4><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); break; default: if (lpt <= 8) { fused_block_forward_kernel<8><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else { fused_block_forward_kernel<16><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>( x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } break; } } ``` But for `lpt=3`, we launch `<4>` which is fine (extra registers unused? But the compiler unrolls MAX_LPT=4, and lpt=3 means we break early in loops. The compiler still allocates 4*8=32 floats. Fine. Similarly, for lpt up to 16, we launch `<16>`. One issue: `cudaFuncSetAttribute` expects the kernel function, not the template instantiation. We need to get the function pointer. In the switch, it might be tricky. We can just set attribute for the max template: ``` cudaFuncSetAttribute(fused_block_forward_kernel<16>, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 164 * 1024); ``` But the attribute must be set before launch. Since `shared_mem` varies per launch, and we might launch `<4>`, setting attribute for `<16>` won't affect `<4>`. Actually, each template instantiation is a different function. The shared memory per kernel can be set per function. The hardware limit is per function. Let's set it for all instantiations we might use. We can write a helper: ``` template <int MAX_LPT> void set_smem_attr(size_t shared_mem) { cudaFuncSetAttribute(fused_block_forward_kernel<MAX_LPT>, cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, shared_mem); } ``` And call it before the switch. But maybe we don't need it if shared_mem <= 48KB for our sizes. For L=4096,d=256, shared_mem = (4096+512 + 256*3*8)*4 = (4608 + 6144)*4 = 10752*4 = 43008 bytes = 42KB. That's less than 48KB. So we don't need `cudaFuncSetAttribute`. Good! For L up to maybe 5500, it would exceed. But L=4096 is fixed. So we can skip the `cudaFuncSetAttribute` and just pass `shared_mem` to the kernel launch. It will be allocated from the dynamic shared memory pool, which has a default max of 48KB per block. 42KB < 48KB. So safe. Great. Now, reduction wrapper: ```cpp void reduce_mean_rstd(const torch::Tensor& x, torch::Tensor& mean, torch::Tensor& rstd) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); int N = C * L; const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); float* m_ptr = mean.data_ptr<float>(); float* r_ptr = rstd.data_ptr<float>(); reduce_mean_rstd_kernel<<<B, 1024>>>(x_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, B, C, L); } ``` Wait, we need to ensure the kernel handles N being large and blockDim.x=1024. The `for` loops use `blockDim.x`. Good. One more thing: float4 alignment. `xb` may not be 16-byte aligned for each batch element `b*N`? Since N = C*L = 1,048,576. `b*N` is a multiple of 1M floats = 4MB. That's certainly divisible by 16 bytes. So `reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(xb)` is safe. Now, Python side: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ mean, float* __restrict__ rstd, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; const float* xb = x + b * N; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; int N4 = N / 4; const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(xb); for (int i = tid; i < N4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 v = x4[i]; sum += v.x + v.y + v.z + v.w; sum_sq += v.x * v.x + v.y * v.y + v.z * v.z + v.w * v.w; } for (int i = N4 * 4 + tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float v = xb[i]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float ssum[32]; __shared__ float ssq[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; if (lane == 0) { ssum[warp_id] = sum; ssq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float ws = (lane < num_warps) ? ssum[lane] : 0.0f; float wsq = (lane < num_warps) ? ssq[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { ws += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, ws, offset); wsq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wsq, offset); } if (lane == 0) { float m = ws / N; float m2 = wsq / N; float var = m2 - m * m; mean[b] = m; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } } template <int MAX_LPT> __global__ void fused_block_forward_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ residual, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ mean, const float* __restrict__ rstd, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int L, int K, int dilation) { const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; const int THREADS = 1024; int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out_tile = blockIdx.y; int c_out_start = c_out_tile * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lpt = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; if (lpt > MAX_LPT) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; int smem_len = L + 2 * dilation; float* in_sm = smem; float* w_sm = smem + smem_len; int w_total = C * K * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; for (int idx = tid; idx < w_total; idx += THREADS) { int c_local = idx % C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tmp = idx / C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int k = tmp % K; int c_in = tmp / K; int c_out = c_out_start + c_local; float wval = 0.0f; if (c_out < C) { wval = weight[((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + k]; } w_sm[idx] = wval; } __syncthreads(); float m = mean[b]; float rs = rstd[b]; int x_base = b * C * L; float acc[MAX_LPT][C_OUT_PER_BLOCK]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < MAX_LPT; ++i) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { acc[i][c] = 0.0f; } } for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { for (int p = tid; p < smem_len; p += THREADS) { int pos = p - dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (pos >= 0 && pos < L) { val = x[x_base + c_in * L + pos]; val = (val - m) * rs; float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); val = val * cdf; } in_sm[p] = val; } __syncthreads(); for (int i = 0; i < lpt; ++i) { int l = tid + i * THREADS; if (l >= L) break; float v0 = in_sm[l + 2 * dilation]; float v1 = in_sm[l + dilation]; float v2 = in_sm[l]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int widx0 = (c_in * K + 0) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; int widx1 = (c_in * K + 1) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; int widx2 = (c_in * K + 2) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; acc[i][c] += w_sm[widx0] * v0 + w_sm[widx1] * v1 + w_sm[widx2] * v2; } } __syncthreads(); } for (int i = 0; i < lpt; ++i) { int l = tid + i * THREADS; if (l >= L) break; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int c_out = c_out_start + c; if (c_out < C) { float val = acc[i][c] + bias[c_out] + residual[x_base + c_out * L + l]; out[x_base + c_out * L + l] = val; } } } } void launch_fused_block_forward(const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& residual, const torch::Tensor& weight, const torch::Tensor& bias, const torch::Tensor& mean, const torch::Tensor& rstd, torch::Tensor& out, int dilation) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); int K = weight.size(2); const int THREADS = 1024; const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; int lpt = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; dim3 grid(B, (C + C_OUT_PER_BLOCK - 1) / C_OUT_PER_BLOCK); dim3 block(THREADS); int smem_len = L + 2 * dilation; int w_size = C * K * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; size_t shared_mem = (smem_len + w_size) * sizeof(float); const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); const float* res_ptr = residual.data_ptr<float>(); const float* w_ptr = weight.data_ptr<float>(); const float* b_ptr = bias.data_ptr<float>(); const float* m_ptr = mean.data_ptr<float>(); const float* r_ptr = rstd.data_ptr<float>(); float* o_ptr = out.data_ptr<float>(); if (lpt <= 1) { fused_block_forward_kernel<1><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else if (lpt <= 2) { fused_block_forward_kernel<2><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else if (lpt <= 4) { fused_block_forward_kernel<4><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else if (lpt <= 8) { fused_block_forward_kernel<8><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else { fused_block_forward_kernel<16><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, w_ptr, b_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } } void launch_reduce_mean_rstd(const torch::Tensor& x, torch::Tensor& mean, torch::Tensor& rstd) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); int N = C * L; const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); float* m_ptr = mean.data_ptr<float>(); float* r_ptr = rstd.data_ptr<float>(); reduce_mean_rstd_kernel<<<B, 1024>>>(x_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, B, C, L); } ''' cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> void launch_reduce_mean_rstd(const torch::Tensor& x, torch::Tensor& mean, torch::Tensor& rstd); void launch_fused_block_forward(const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& residual, const torch::Tensor& weight, const torch::Tensor& bias, const torch::Tensor& mean, const torch::Tensor& rstd, torch::Tensor& out, int dilation); Now, what about `dilation` in PyTorch? In `Conv1d`, the formula is: output length = floor((L + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_size-1) - 1) / stride) + 1. With stride=1, padding=dilation, kernel_size=3: `L + 2*d - d*(2) - 1 + 1 = L`. Good. Is there any difference in how PyTorch handles the padding compared to our kernel? We pad with zeros outside [0, L-1]. That's correct. Now, let's write the Python code. We need to be careful with `load_inline`. The `cuda_sources` string should contain the CUDA/C++ code. The `cpp_sources` can be empty or contain includes. But we need to specify `functions=['launch_reduce_mean_rstd', 'launch_fused_block_forward']`. Also, we need to specify extra CUDA flags if needed. For example, `--use_fast_math` might make `erff` approximate. We should not use it, or use default. `erff` is standard. One issue: `load_inline` may need `with_cuda=True`. Actually, by default, if `cuda_sources` is provided, it compiles with CUDA. Let's check `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` signature: ``` load_inline(name, cpp_sources, cuda_sources=None, functions=None, extra_cflags=None, extra_cuda_cflags=None, extra_ldflags=None, extra_include_paths=None, build_directory=None, verbose=False, with_cuda=None, is_python_module=True, keep_intermediates=True) ``` We'll call it at module level. ``` cuda_ext = load_inline( name="fused_dilated_conv", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["launch_reduce_mean_rstd", "launch_fused_block_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["--expt-relaxed-constexpr", "-O3"], ) ``` But there is a subtlety: when `cpp_sources` is empty, does it work? We might need to at least include `<torch/extension.h>` in `cpp_sources`. Or we can put everything in `cuda_sources`. Let's include the host wrappers in `cuda_sources`. Since nvcc compiles `.cu` files, it handles both device and host code. However, `load_inline` might not auto-generate pybind11 bindings correctly if the functions are defined in `cuda_sources`. I think it should; it compiles all sources together. To be safe, we can put the declarations in `cpp_sources`: ``` cpp_sources = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> void launch_reduce_mean_rstd(const torch::Tensor& x, torch::Tensor& mean, torch::Tensor& rstd); void launch_fused_block_forward(const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& residual, const torch::Tensor& norm_w, const torch::Tensor& norm_b, const torch::Tensor& conv_w, const torch::Tensor& conv_b, const torch::Tensor& mean, const torch::Tensor& rstd, torch::Tensor& out, int dilation); ''' ``` And define them in `cuda_sources`. The `functions` list tells `load_inline` which functions to bind. Actually, `load_inline` auto-generates a `PYBIND11_MODULE` that calls `m.def` for each function in `functions`. It expects the functions to be declared in the combined translation unit. So having declarations in `cpp_sources` or `cuda_sources` is fine. Let's combine everything into `cuda_sources` and leave `cpp_sources` empty. Wait, if `cpp_sources` is empty, the generated pybind11 module still needs the function declarations. But if the functions are defined in `cuda_sources`, they are visible. I think it's okay. To be safe, I'll put everything in `cuda_sources`. Now, check the GELU formula: PyTorch uses `0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))`. Our code: `float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); val = val * cdf;`. That's equivalent. One more nuance: the order of operations in LayerNorm. `y = (x - mean) * rstd`. Then `y = y * weight + bias`. We do `val = (val - m) * rs; val = val * norm_w + norm_b;`. Correct. Now, write the final code. But first, compile and test. We need to consider that `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` will compile the code at import time. If there are compile errors, we need to fix them. Let's draft the code carefully. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ mean, float* __restrict__ rstd, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; const float* xb = x + b * N; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; int N4 = N / 4; const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(xb); for (int i = tid; i < N4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 v = x4[i]; sum += v.x + v.y + v.z + v.w; sum_sq += v.x * v.x + v.y * v.y + v.z * v.z + v.w * v.w; } for (int i = N4 * 4 + tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float v = xb[i]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float ssum[32]; __shared__ float ssq[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; if (lane == 0) { ssum[warp_id] = sum; ssq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float ws = (lane < num_warps) ? ssum[lane] : 0.0f; float wsq = (lane < num_warps) ? ssq[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { ws += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, ws, offset); wsq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wsq, offset); } if (lane == 0) { float m = ws / N; float m2 = wsq / N; float var = m2 - m * m; mean[b] = m; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } } template <int MAX_LPT> __global__ void fused_block_forward_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ residual, const float* __restrict__ norm_w, const float* __restrict__ norm_b, const float* __restrict__ conv_w, const float* __restrict__ conv_b, const float* __restrict__ mean, const float* __restrict__ rstd, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int C, int L, int K, int dilation) { const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; const int THREADS = 1024; int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out_tile = blockIdx.y; int c_out_start = c_out_tile * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lpt = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; if (lpt > MAX_LPT) return; extern __shared__ float smem[]; int smem_len = L + 2 * dilation; float* in_sm = smem; float* w_sm = smem + smem_len; int w_total = C * K * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; for (int idx = tid; idx < w_total; idx += THREADS) { int c_local = idx % C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tmp = idx / C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int k = tmp % K; int c_in = tmp / K; int c_out = c_out_start + c_local; float wval = 0.0f; if (c_out < C) { wval = conv_w[((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + k]; } w_sm[idx] = wval; } __syncthreads(); float m = mean[b]; float rs = rstd[b]; int x_base = b * C * L; float acc[MAX_LPT][C_OUT_PER_BLOCK]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < MAX_LPT; ++i) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { acc[i][c] = 0.0f; } } for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { for (int p = tid; p < smem_len; p += THREADS) { int pos = p - dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (pos >= 0 && pos < L) { val = x[x_base + c_in * L + pos]; val = (val - m) * rs; val = val * norm_w[c_in * L + pos] + norm_b[c_in * L + pos]; float cdf = 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); val = val * cdf; } in_sm[p] = val; } __syncthreads(); for (int i = 0; i < lpt; ++i) { int l = tid + i * THREADS; if (l >= L) break; float v0 = in_sm[l + 2 * dilation]; float v1 = in_sm[l + dilation]; float v2 = in_sm[l]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int widx0 = (c_in * K + 0) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; int widx1 = (c_in * K + 1) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; int widx2 = (c_in * K + 2) * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK + c; acc[i][c] += w_sm[widx0] * v0 + w_sm[widx1] * v1 + w_sm[widx2] * v2; } } __syncthreads(); } for (int i = 0; i < lpt; ++i) { int l = tid + i * THREADS; if (l >= L) break; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int c_out = c_out_start + c; if (c_out < C) { float val = acc[i][c] + conv_b[c_out] + residual[x_base + c_out * L + l]; out[x_base + c_out * L + l] = val; } } } } void launch_reduce_mean_rstd(const torch::Tensor& x, torch::Tensor& mean, torch::Tensor& rstd) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); float* m_ptr = mean.data_ptr<float>(); float* r_ptr = rstd.data_ptr<float>(); reduce_mean_rstd_kernel<<<B, 1024>>>(x_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, B, C, L); } void launch_fused_block_forward(const torch::Tensor& x, const torch::Tensor& residual, const torch::Tensor& norm_w, const torch::Tensor& norm_b, const torch::Tensor& conv_w, const torch::Tensor& conv_b, const torch::Tensor& mean, const torch::Tensor& rstd, torch::Tensor& out, int dilation) { int B = x.size(0); int C = x.size(1); int L = x.size(2); int K = conv_w.size(2); const int THREADS = 1024; const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; int lpt = (L + THREADS - 1) / THREADS; dim3 grid(B, (C + C_OUT_PER_BLOCK - 1) / C_OUT_PER_BLOCK); dim3 block(THREADS); int smem_len = L + 2 * dilation; int w_size = C * K * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; size_t shared_mem = (smem_len + w_size) * sizeof(float); const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); const float* res_ptr = residual.data_ptr<float>(); const float* nw_ptr = norm_w.data_ptr<float>(); const float* nb_ptr = norm_b.data_ptr<float>(); const float* cw_ptr = conv_w.data_ptr<float>(); const float* cb_ptr = conv_b.data_ptr<float>(); const float* m_ptr = mean.data_ptr<float>(); const float* r_ptr = rstd.data_ptr<float>(); float* o_ptr = out.data_ptr<float>(); if (lpt <= 1) { fused_block_forward_kernel<1><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, nw_ptr, nb_ptr, cw_ptr, cb_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else if (lpt <= 2) { fused_block_forward_kernel<2><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, nw_ptr, nb_ptr, cw_ptr, cb_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else if (lpt <= 4) { fused_block_forward_kernel<4><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, nw_ptr, nb_ptr, cw_ptr, cb_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else if (lpt <= 8) { fused_block_forward_kernel<8><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, nw_ptr, nb_ptr, cw_ptr, cb_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } else { fused_block_forward_kernel<16><<<grid, block, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, res_ptr, nw_ptr, nb_ptr, cw_ptr, cb_ptr, m_ptr, r_ptr, o_ptr, B, C, L, K, dilation); } } ''' ``` Wait, we need to make sure `norm_w` and `norm_b` have shape `(C, L)`. In PyTorch, `nn.LayerNorm([C, L])` weight shape is `(C, L)`. So `norm_w[c_in * L + pos]` is correct. Good. Now, the Python side: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, channels, seq_len, kernel_size, max_dilation_power): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.channels = channels self.seq_len = seq_len self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.max_dilation_power = max_dilation_power self.norms = nn.ModuleList() self.convs = nn.ModuleList() for i in range(max_dilation_power + 1): dilation = 2 ** i self.norms.append(nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len])) self.convs.append(nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation)) def forward(self, x): x = x.contiguous() residual = x curr = x B, C, L = curr.shape for i in range(self.max_dilation_power + 1): dilation = 2 ** i norm = self.norms[i] conv = self.convs[i] mean = torch.empty(B, device=curr.device, dtype=curr.dtype) rstd = torch.empty(B, device=curr.device, dtype=curr.dtype) cuda_ext.launch_reduce_mean_rstd(curr, mean, rstd) out = torch.empty_like(curr) cuda_ext.launch_fused_block_forward( curr, residual, norm.weight, norm.bias, conv.weight, conv.bias, mean, rstd, out, dilation ) curr = out return curr ``` We need `cuda_ext` defined. Let's put `cuda_ext = load_inline(...)` at module level. One more thought: `launch_reduce_mean_rstd` uses `B` blocks of 1024 threads. But if `B` is large (like 32), it's okay. If `B` is small and `C*L` is large, we could use many blocks per batch. But B is 32. Now, before we compile, let's think about potential syntax issues in the CUDA code. Issue 1: We used `float4`. The pointer `x4` must be aligned. We assume aligned. But `blockDim.x` is 1024. The warp shuffle uses `__shfl_down_sync`. We need `#include <cuda_runtime.h>`. Issue 2: We used `erff`. Need `<cmath>`? CUDA math provides `erff`. It's in `math.h` and `cmath`. We included `<cmath>`. Issue 3: The `extern __shared__ float smem[];` inside a template. That's fine. Issue 4: `w_sm` indexing: `w_sm[idx]`. Since `w_sm = smem + smem_len`. `w_sm` is a `float*`. Good. Issue 5: `for (int i = N4 * 4 + tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x)` in reduction. If N4 * 4 == N, the start index is N + tid, which is >= N, so the loop body never executes. That's fine. Issue 6: `reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(xb)`. If `xb` is not aligned to 16 bytes, this is undefined. But PyTorch tensors are aligned to at least 256 bytes for the base pointer. `xb = x + b*N`. N = C*L. For C=256, L=4096, N=1,048,576. b*N floats = b*4,194,304 bytes. This is a multiple of 16. Even if L is not a multiple of 4, the offset for the next batch may not be aligned? Wait, if C*L is not a multiple of 4, `b*N` may not be a multiple of 4 floats (16 bytes). For example, C=3, L=5, N=15. Then b*N = 15, 30, etc. 15*4 = 60 bytes, not divisible by 16. In that case, float4 cast is invalid. But for our case, N = 1,048,576 is divisible by 4. However, to be generic, we could check alignment. We can add: ``` bool aligned = (reinterpret_cast<uintptr_t>(xb) % 16 == 0); if (aligned) { ... float4 ... } else { ... scalar ... } ``` But to keep it simple, we can just do scalar loads. The reduction is a tiny fraction of total time. Scalar loads might be fine. But we can also add alignment check. Actually, since C*L is huge and likely multiple of 4, alignment holds. For safety, I'll handle the general case: if `reinterpret_cast<uintptr_t>(xb) % 16 == 0`, use float4; else scalar. But doing this per block is trivial. Let's replace the float4 loop with a version that checks alignment. ``` bool aligned = (reinterpret_cast<uintptr_t>(xb) % 16 == 0); if (aligned) { int N4 = N / 4; const float4* x4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(xb); for (int i = tid; i < N4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 v = x4[i]; ... } for (int i = N4*4 + tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { ... } } else { for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { ... } } ``` But this adds a branch per block. Fine. Actually, let's just use a generic scalar loop. The reduction is only 134 MB read per block. 9 times = 1.2 GB. At 1.5 TB/s, that's 0.8 ms. Scalar loads might take 2-3 ms. Still negligible compared to conv time. Let's keep scalar to avoid alignment issues. But we can vectorize if we want. Wait, scalar loads with 1024 threads each doing 1024 iterations: each thread does 1024 loads. 1024 threads per block. Memory throughput may be lower than optimal. But 32 blocks total. 32 * 1024 threads = 32768 threads. That's enough to saturate memory. So scalar is fine. I'll remove float4 to simplify and avoid alignment issues. The reduction kernel becomes: ```cpp __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ mean, float* __restrict__ rstd, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; const float* xb = x + b * N; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float v = xb[i]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float ssum[32]; __shared__ float ssq[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; if (lane == 0) { ssum[warp_id] = sum; ssq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float ws = (lane < num_warps) ? ssum[lane] : 0.0f; float wsq = (lane < num_warps) ? ssq[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { ws += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, ws, offset); wsq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wsq, offset); } if (lane == 0) { float m = ws / N; float m2 = wsq / N; float var = m2 - m * m; mean[b] = m; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } } ``` This is clean. Now, potential numerical issue: we compute `var = m2 - m*m`. If mean is large, this can be negative due to rounding. We should clamp: `var = fmaxf(m2 - m*m, 0.0f);`. PyTorch does something similar? Actually, PyTorch's LayerNorm computes variance with Welford, which avoids negative variance. To be safe, we can clamp to 0. Let's add `var = fmaxf(var, 0.0f);` before `rsqrtf`. Now, one more nuance: the `residual` in the model is the original input before the loop. In the reference code: ``` residual = x for ... x = norm(x) x = activation(x) x = conv(x) x = x + residual return x ``` So after the first block, `x` becomes `conv(norm(gelu(x))) + residual`. For the second block, `x` is this new tensor. `residual` remains the original input. Our code replicates this: `curr` is updated each iteration, `residual` stays as initial `x`. Good. Now, compile the code. Let's try to compile with `compile_kernel`. But before that, we need to make sure the Python code is a complete module. We'll write it as a single string and pass to compile_kernel. Actually, `compile_kernel` expects the full Python source. It compiles and checks for errors. Let's do that.

turn 1llm latency: 19.9s
reasoning
The problem is `cpp_sources=""`, leaving main.cpp empty with no declarations. `load_inline` auto-generates pybind from `functions=[...]` using `torch::wrap_pybind_function`, but it needs `cpp_sources` to *declare* those functions so the binding code compiles. Also note that we don't want to define `PYBIND11_MODULE` ourselves, but we DO need to supply C++ declarations in the `cpp_sources` string. The `cpp_sources` string is compiled as `main.cpp`, and `cuda_sources` compiled as `cuda.cu`. Then the linker links them. So I should write the C++ function declarations in `cpp_sources`.

It might be just barely outside tolerance due to fast math erff approximations or slightly different GELU computation. Let's think: In the reference, Conv1d uses `padding=dilation`. For kernel_size=3 and dilation=d: input indices used: l - d, l, l + d (since padding=d on both sides). Wait, for PyTorch Conv1d with padding=dilation, the output length matches input length because we pad d on each side. For kernel_size=3 and dilation=d, the convolution accesses: weight[0] * input[l - d] weight[1] * input[l] weight[2] * input[l + d] BUT indexed according to the kernel index mapping. In PyTorch Conv1d, the weights are stored as [C_out, C_in, K]. The computation for output position l is: sum_{k=0}^{K-1} weight[c_out, c_in, k] * input[c_in, l + k*d - pad]? Actually the standard formula for dilated conv1d: out[l] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} weight[k] * input[l + (k - (K-1)/2)*d]? No, that's for symmetric padding. With padding=d on left and right: out[n] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} weight[k] * input[n * stride - pad + k*d] (stride=1). So for n from 0 to L-1: index = n - d + k*d = n + (k-1)*d. So: k=0 => n - d k=1 => n k=2 => n + d. Yes, that matches my indexing if we consider `v0` = in_sm[l] = pos - d? Wait. In my code: in_sm index p maps to position pos = p - dilation. Then I do l = tid + i*THREADS. v0 = in_sm[l] = pos = l - dilation. v1 = in_sm[l + dilation] = pos = l. v2 = in_sm[l + 2*dilation] = pos = l + dilation. And weights: widx0 corresponds to k=0, widx1 to k=1, widx2 to k=2. So output = w[0]*input[l-d] + w[1]*input[l] + w[2]*input[l+d]. This matches PyTorch Conv1d with padding=dilation and kernel_size=3. So the indexing is correct. The GELU approximation: `0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))`. Reference PyTorch uses tanh approximation for GELU by default in CUDA, but numerically close to the exact one. With `--use_fast_math`, `erff` is probably a fast approximation. The max diff is ~0.0007, atol=1e-4. It's failing by a factor of ~7. We could remove `--use_fast_math` for more accurate `erff`, but that will slow it down slightly. Let's see if removing it gets within tolerance. Also check LayerNorm: The reference uses `nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len])` which normalizes over the last two dimensions (per batch element). In my code, I compute mean and rstd over the entire C*L elements per batch, which matches. Residual is added from the very first input (original x). In my code, `residual` is set to `x` before the loop and stays constant. The reference also sets `residual = x` before the loop and adds it each time. Yes, `residual` is not updated. My code uses `residual = x` at the start and uses it for all iterations. This is correct. Wait, but in the first iteration, `curr` is `x`, `residual` is `x`, so out = block(x) + x. Then `curr = out`. In the second iteration, `curr` is out, but `residual` is still original x. This matches. I'll use the tanh approximation to match PyTorch exactly: ```python 0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) ``` This is what PyTorch's GELU uses internally. Let's compute it more accurately in CUDA. GELU(x) = 0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(k * (x + 0.044715 * x^3))) where k = sqrt(2/pi) ≈ 0.7978845608. In CUDA: float x3 = val * val * val; float t = tanhf(0.7978845608f * (val + 0.044715f * x3)); val = 0.5f * val * (1.0f + t); This should match PyTorch more closely. Another issue: `x = x.contiguous()` is done at the start. `curr` is contiguous. Good. One more thing: In PyTorch, Conv1d outputs are computed with a bias for each output channel. Let me rewrite the reduce kernel: ```cuda __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd_kernel(const float* x, float* mean, float* rstd, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; const float* xb = x + b * N; // First pass: compute sum float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { sum += xb[i]; } // Reduce sum ... __shared__ float smean; if (threadIdx.x == 0) smean = reduced_sum / N; __syncthreads(); // Second pass: compute sum of squared deviations float ss = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float diff = xb[i] - smean; ss += diff * diff; } // Reduce ss ... if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float var = reduced_ss / N; mean[b] = smean; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } ``` This two-pass algorithm is more numerically stable for variance, though it requires an extra loop over memory. For B=32 and N=1M, each block processes 1M elements. With 1024 threads, each thread reads ~1000 elements. Two passes means ~2000 reads per thread. This is still fine for H100. PyTorch computes mean very carefully. For N=1M and values ~N(0,1), sum is ~1000 and N is 1M. The mean is ~0.001. The absolute rounding error in computing mean via sum/N for fp32 could be on the order of machine_epsilon * |sum| / N ≈ 1e-7 * 1000 / 1e6 = 1e-10, which is negligible. However, the variance computation via E[x^2] - E[x]^2 could have catastrophic cancellation. E[x^2] ≈ 1, E[x]^2 ≈ 1e-6 (for a sample mean of ~0.001). So var ≈ 1 - 1e-6. Computing 1 - 1e-6 in fp32 is fine - you get 0.999999. The relative error could be around 1e-7 * 1 = 1e-7. Let me think about Conv1d in PyTorch stepping through the positions: For `conv = nn.Conv1d(C, C, K=3, padding=d, dilation=d)`, when the input `x` is [B, C, L]: PyTorch internally calls something like: ```python output = F.conv1d(input, weight, bias, stride=1, padding=d, dilation=d) ``` The `F.conv1d` padding behavior adds `d` zeros to the left and `d` zeros to the right. The formula is: output[b, c_out, i] = bias[c_out] + sum_{c_in=0}^{C-1} sum_{k=0}^{2} weight[c_out, c_in, k] * input[b, c_in, i + k*d - d] For k=0,1,2: input[b, c_in, i-d], input[b, c_in, i], input[b, c_in, i+d] So for each output position i, the kernel samples are at i-d, i, i+d (with appropriate padding). In my kernel: `in_sm[p]` stores value at position `pos = p - d`, where `p` ranges from `0` to `L+2d-1`. So `in_sm[0]` stores input at `-d` (which is 0), `in_sm[d]` stores input at `0`, `in_sm[L+d-1]` stores input at `L-1`, `in_sm[L+2d-1]` stores input at `L+d-1` (which is 0). For thread computing output at position `l` (where `l` ranges from `0` to `L-1`): v0 = in_sm[l] => position l-d v1 = in_sm[l+d] => position l v2 = in_sm[l+2d] => position l+d And the accumulation is: w[k=0] * v0 + w[k=1] * v1 + w[k=2] * v2 = w[0] * input[l-d] + w[1] * input[l] + w[2] * input[l+d] This matches PyTorch perfectly. Actually, conv1d with padding in PyTorch computes the output at each position as the sum over the kernel. For a kernel of size 3, dilation=d: output[i] = w[0] * input[i] + w[1] * input[i+d] + w[2] * input[i+2d] Wait! There's a subtlety with padding. The reference uses: ```python nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation) ``` In PyTorch, `padding=dilation` means we add `dilation` zeros on EACH side. So for kernel_size=3 and dilation=d, the output length depends on the padding mode. With padding=d and kernel_size=3 and stride=1, the output size formula for conv1d is: L_out = floor((L + 2*d - d*(K-1) - 1) / 1) + 1 = L + 2*d - d*(K-1) = L + 2d - 2d = L So the output is the same length L. But importantly, the conv accesses input positions from 0 - d to L - 1 + d. With padding=d, the negative indices (up to -d) are filled with zeros, and indices >= L are also filled with zeros. So for output position l: - conv accesses input[l], input[l+d], input[l+2d] — but wait, with padding=d on both sides, it's more subtle. Actually, for Conv1d with padding=p: The input is conceptually padded with p zeros on each side. For kernel_size=k, dilation=d: The receptive field is at indices: [0, d, 2d] relative to the kernel center. With padding=p=d, the first output corresponds to the kernel centered at position 0 of the original input (with d zeros to the left). For dilation d: - The input can be viewed as d interleaved sequences - Each sequence has length ceil(L/d) or floor(L/d) - A dilated conv with dilation=d and kernel_size=3 on the original sequence is equivalent to a standard conv with kernel_size=3 on each interleaved sequence. Proof: For output position l, the dilated conv uses input[l-d], input[l], input[l+d]. For the interleaved sequence containing position l (i.e., positions l % d, l % d + d, l % d + 2d, ...), position l is at index l // d in its sequence. The dilated conv uses: - input[l-d] = the element before l in the same interleaved sequence (if l >= d) - input[l] = the current element in the interleaved sequence - input[l+d] = the element after l in the same interleaved sequence (if l+d < L) With padding=d on both sides, each interleaved sequence has padding=1 on both sides. If I eliminate the weight shared memory and load weights directly from global memory: ```cuda for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { // load activation to shared memory for (int p = tid; p < smem_len; p += THREADS) { ... in_sm[p] = ...; } __syncthreads(); // compute conv using global memory weights for (int i = 0; i < lpt; ++i) { int l = tid + i * THREADS; if (l >= L) break; float v0 = in_sm[l]; float v1 = in_sm[l + d]; float v2 = in_sm[l + 2*d]; for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int c_out = c_out_start + c; if (c_out < C) { int widx0 = ((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + 0; int widx1 = ((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + 1; int widx2 = ((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + 2; acc[i][c] += conv_w[widx0] * v0 + conv_w[widx1] * v1 + conv_w[widx2] * v2; } } } __syncthreads(); } ``` This removes the weight shared memory entirely! Now shared memory is just `smem_len` floats. For L=4096, d=256: smem_len = 4608 floats = 18432 bytes. This is well under 48KB. The tradeoff is that loading weights from global memory is slower than loading from shared memory. But for 256 iterations * 3 loads per iteration * 8 channels = 6144 weight loads per thread, and with good cache locality (consecutive threads access consecutive weights), it might still be acceptable. Actually, consecutive threads access the SAME weights! Because `c_out_start` is the same for all threads in a block, and `c_in` is the same in the loop. The weight indices `widx0, widx1, widx2` depend on `c_out` (which loops 0..7) and `c_in`, but NOT on `tid`. So all threads in a block access the same weight values simultaneously. This means the weights will be cached in L1, and subsequent accesses will be fast. In fact, the first access might be broadcast to all threads, which is efficient. So using global memory for weights might be almost as fast as shared memory in this case, because of the broadcast pattern! And by removing the weight shared memory, I reduce shared memory usage and potential issues. Let me rewrite the kernel with this simplification. Let me rewrite the fully-fused kernel without weight shared memory and see if that helps with stability. Also, I'll make the reduction kernel simpler, using a two-level reduction but without potential for issues. For the reduction kernel: ```cuda __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd_kernel(const float* x, float* mean, float* rstd, int B, int C, int L) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* sdata = smem; int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; const float* xb = x + b * N; // Each thread computes partial sum float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float v = xb[i]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } sdata[tid] = sum; sdata[tid + blockDim.x] = sum_sq; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction in shared memory for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + stride]; sdata[tid + blockDim.x] += sdata[tid + blockDim.x + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (tid == 0) { float m = sdata[0] / N; float m2 = sdata[blockDim.x] / N; float var = m2 - m * m; if (var < 0.0f) var = 0.0f; mean[b] = m; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } ``` This is a much cleaner reduction. Shared memory size: `2 * blockDim.x` floats. For blockDim.x = 1024: 2048 floats = 8KB. And for the main kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_block_forward_kernel( const float* x, const float* residual, const float* norm_w, const float* norm_b, const float* conv_w, const float* conv_b, const float* mean, const float* rstd, float* out, int B, int C, int L, int K, int dilation) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out_start = blockIdx.y * C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float in_sm[]; int smem_len = L + 2 * dilation; float m = mean[b]; float rs = rstd[b]; int x_base = b * C * L; float acc[4][C_OUT_PER_BLOCK]; // for lpt <= 4 // initialize acc to 0 for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { // Load activations into shared memory for (int p = tid; p < smem_len; p += THREADS) { int pos = p - dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (pos >= 0 && pos < L) { val = x[x_base + c_in * L + pos]; val = (val - m) * rs; val = val * norm_w[c_in * L + pos] + norm_b[c_in * L + pos]; val = val * 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); } in_sm[p] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute partial conv products for (int l = tid; l < L; l += THREADS) { float v0 = in_sm[l]; float v1 = in_sm[l + dilation]; float v2 = in_sm[l + 2 * dilation]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int c_out = c_out_start + c; if (c_out < C) { int widx = ((c_out * C) + c_in) * K; acc[l / THREADS][c] += conv_w[widx + 0] * v0 + conv_w[widx + 1] * v1 + conv_w[widx + 2] * v2; } } } __syncthreads(); } // Write output for (int l = tid; l < L; l += THREADS) { for (int c = 0; c < C_OUT_PER_BLOCK; ++c) { int c_out = c_out_start + c; if (c_out < C) { out[x_base + c_out * L + l] = acc[l / THREADS][c] + conv_b[c_out] + residual[x_base + c_out * L + l]; } } } } ``` Wait, the `acc` array size isn't constant if `l / THREADS` varies. For L=4096 and THREADS=1024, l ranges 0..4095 in increments of 1024. `l / 1024` can be 0, 1, 2, 3. But in C++, integer division is fine. However, `acc` needs to be sized at compile time. I can just use `acc[4][8]` since lpt is at most 4. Actually, let me just use the dynamic loop approach rather than trying to unroll. The performance might be slightly worse but the code is simpler and less error-prone. ```cuda float acc[16]; // max 16 positions per thread * up to 8 channels for (int i = 0; i < 16 * 8; ++i) acc[i] = 0.0f; ``` This is getting messy. What if I just write a simpler kernel where each thread computes ONE output position and ONE output channel? Then each thread handles a single scalar value and there's no need for complex accumulation arrays. ```cuda __global__ void fused_block_forward_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y; int l = blockIdx.z * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (l >= L || c_out >= C) return; float m = mean[b]; float rs = rstd[b]; int x_base = b * C * L; float sum = conv_b[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int pos = l + (k - 1) * dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (pos >= 0 && pos < L) { val = x[x_base + c_in * L + pos]; val = (val - m) * rs; val = val * norm_w[c_in * L + pos] + norm_b[c_in * L + pos]; val = val * 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); } float w = conv_w[((c_out * C) + c_in) * K + k]; sum += w * val; } } sum += residual[x_base + c_out * L + l]; out[x_base + c_out * L + l] = sum; } ``` This is MUCH simpler! But what's the performance? Each thread computes one output element. Threads in a block process consecutive positions l for a fixed c_out and batch. The grid: (B, C, ceil(L / 1024)) = (32, 256, 4) = 32768 blocks. Each block has 1024 threads, so total threads = 32 * 256 * 4096 = 33.5M threads. Wait, each thread computes one output element. For each element, it iterates over c_in (256) and k (3), doing 768 iterations. Each iteration reads input, norm weights/bias, and conv weight from global memory. Global memory loads: - For each c_in, k: input value from x (coalesced within a warp since consecutive l) - For each c_in, k: norm_w, norm_b (same for all threads in a warp, broadcast) - For each c_in, k: conv_w (same for all threads in a warp, broadcast) So consecutive threads in a warp read consecutive input elements (coalesced), and the weights are broadcast (cached in L1). This is actually quite efficient! The main issue is that each thread does 768 iterations, but the compute is simple. At 33.5M threads * 768 iterations ≈ 25B loads/cycles. On H100, the memory bandwidth is ~3TB/s. Each load is 4 bytes. Total memory traffic: - Input x: each output reads 3 * C input elements = 768 elements. Total input reads: 33.5M * 768 * 4 bytes ≈ 103GB - Norm weights/biases: read 2*C times per block, but cached. Total: minimal. - Conv weights: read C*3 times per block, cached. Total: minimal. - Residual: read once per output: 33.5M * 4 = 134MB - Output: write once: 134MB Total memory traffic is dominated by input reads: ~103GB. At 3TB/s, this takes ~34ms. That's very slow! Wait, that can't be right. Not all input reads are from global memory; they should be L1 cached. For consecutive positions l, l+1, l+2, the inputs overlap by 2 positions per k when considering adjacent l values. Actually, the issue is that for fixed c_out, all threads in a block read the SAME conv weights. For thread l and thread l+1, they read input at position l and l+1 respectively, which are consecutive. But for each c_in, they need to read 3 positions spread out by dilation. For dilation=1: positions l-1, l, l+1. Adjacent threads l and l+1 read overlapping positions [l-1,l,l+1] and [l,l+1,l+2]. With a cache line of 128 bytes (32 floats), adjacent threads share cache lines for positions l and l+1. So L1 cache helps a lot. For dilation=256: positions l-256, l, l+256. Adjacent threads l and l+1 read positions that are far apart. They share no cache lines. So each thread does 3 cold global memory reads per c_in. For dilation=256 and C=256, each thread does 768 reads, each 4 bytes. Total reads per thread = 3KB. With 1024 threads per block, each block reads ~3MB. At 3TB/s, a block takes ~1µs. With 32768 blocks total and 132 SMs, each SM processes ~248 blocks. Total time ~248µs per SM. That's about 0.25ms for the whole kernel per layer. Wait, that's actually not bad! 0.25ms per layer * 9 layers = 2.25ms. If the reference takes ~3-5ms, this might be competitive! For smaller dilations, the cache locality is better, so it would be even faster. And this kernel is dead simple - no shared memory, no syncthreads, no potential for hangs. Let me write this version! So all threads in the block compute the same c_out but different l. All threads read the SAME conv weights. So the weights will be heavily broadcasted and L1 cached. Similarly for norm weights/biases, all threads read the same values for a given c_in and position. So global memory access pattern: 1. Coalesced reads for input x[b, c_in, l + k*dilation] 2. Broadcast reads for norm_w, norm_b 3. Broadcast reads for conv_w This is very cache-friendly. Let me write this simplified kernel. For the reduction kernel, I'll also write a simple and safe version. Actually, I can even skip writing a custom reduction kernel and use `torch.mean` and `torch.var`. But a custom kernel for mean and rstd is simple enough. ```cuda __global__ void reduce_mean_rstd_kernel(const float* x, float* mean, float* rstd, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; const float* xb = x + b * N; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float v = xb[i]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } // Warp reduction for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float ssum[32]; __shared__ float ssq[32]; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; if (lane == 0) { ssum[warp_id] = sum; ssq[warp_id] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float ws = (lane < num_warps) ? ssum[lane] : 0.0f; float wsq = (lane < num_warps) ? ssq[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { ws += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, ws, offset); wsq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, wsq, offset); } if (lane == 0) { float m = ws / N; float m2 = wsq / N; float var = m2 - m * m; if (var < 0.0f) var = 0.0f; mean[b] = m; rstd[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } } ``` This is the same reduction as before but should work fine. The issues with the previous approach might not have been in the reduction kernel. For the main kernel, the simple per-element approach: ```cuda __global__ void fused_block_forward_kernel( const float* x, const float* residual, const float* norm_w, const float* norm_b, const float* conv_w, const float* conv_b, const float* mean, const float* rstd, float* out, int B, int C, int L, int K, int dilation) { int b = blockIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.y; int l = blockIdx.z * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (l >= L || c_out >= C) return; float m = mean[b]; float rs = rstd[b]; int x_base = b * C * L; float sum = conv_b[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C; ++c_in) { int x_off = x_base + c_in * L; int nw_off = c_in * L; int w_off = (c_out * C + c_in) * K; for (int k = 0; k < K; ++k) { int pos = l + (k - 1) * dilation; float val = 0.0f; if (pos >= 0 && pos < L) { val = x[x_off + pos]; val = (val - m) * rs; val = val * norm_w[nw_off + pos] + norm_b[nw_off + pos]; val = val * 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(val * 0.70710678118f)); } sum += conv_w[w_off + k] * val; } } sum += residual[x_base + c_out * L + l]; out[x_base + c_out * L + l] = sum; } ``` This is clean and simple. Grid: (B, C, ceil(L / THREADS)). For L=4096 and THREADS=1024: ceil(4096/1024) = 4. Grid: (32, 256, 4) = 32768 blocks. Block: 1024 threads. Total threads: 32768 * 1024 = 33.5M threads. Each thread does C*K = 768 iterations of the inner loop. This might be slower than my shared-memory version for small dilations (where shared memory helps with cache locality), but for large dilations (where memory access is strided anyway), it's probably competitive. And more importantly, it's very unlikely to hang! Let me write this version and test it. Actually, I just want to double-check the indexing: `pos = l + (k - 1) * dilation` For k=0: pos = l - dilation For k=1: pos = l For k=2: pos = l + dilation In PyTorch Conv1d with padding=dilation, output position l accesses input positions l-dilation, l, l+dilation (with zeros outside [0, L-1]). My code checks `if (pos >= 0 && pos < L)` and sets val=0 otherwise. This matches exactly. Also, `conv_w[w_off + k]` corresponds to `conv_w[c_out, c_in, k]`. `w_off = (c_out * C + c_in) * K` `w_off + k = c_out * C * K + c_in * K + k` This matches PyTorch's weight layout. And `norm_w[nw_off + pos]` corresponds to `norm_w[c_in, pos]`. `nw_off = c_in * L` `nw_off + pos = c_in * L + pos` This matches since norm weight shape is (C, L). Total per output: 6*C norm values + 3*C conv_w values + 3*C input values + 1 residual. At 256*3*3 = 2304 reads per output, each 4 bytes = ~9KB per output. For 33.5M outputs = ~300GB of memory reads. There are C*K = 768 conv_w values per block, read by all 1024 threads. These are heavily reused in L1 cache. - The norm_w and norm_b for positions l-d, l, l+d are read by threads at positions l. For adjacent threads (l and l+1), the norm values overlap: thread l reads norm at l-d, l, l+d; thread l+1 reads norm at l+1-d, l+1, l+1+d. Only if d=1 do they overlap significantly. The custom kernel: ```cuda __global__ void norm_gelu_kernel( const float* x, const float* mean, const float* rstd, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* out, int N) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; // x shape is (B, C, L) // mean/rstd shape is (B, 1, 1) // weight/bias shape is (C, L) // Need to compute batch and channel for this idx // Actually, the shapes make this tricky. Let me assume x is flattened to (B, C*L) // mean is per batch element, weight/bias are per (C, L) position } ``` Wait, the mean is per batch. For x shape (B, C, L), the flattened index maps to: b = idx / (C * L) c = (idx % (C * L)) / L l = idx % L Then: mean_val = mean[b, 0, 0] rstd_val = rstd[b, 0, 0] weight_val = weight[c, l] bias_val = bias[c, l] The custom kernel: ```cuda int b = idx / (C * L); int rem = idx % (C * L); int c = rem / L; int l = rem % L; float v = x[idx]; v = (v - mean[b]) * rstd[b]; v = v * weight[c * L + l] + bias[c * L + l]; v = v * 0.5f * (1.0f + erff(v * 0.70710678118f)); out[idx] = v; ``` This is very simple! Read residual: 256*4096 = 4MB per block 5. Write output: 4MB per block Total per block: ~20MB. At 3TB/s, this is ~7 microseconds per block. With 1024 blocks (32 batches * 32 channel tiles), total time ~7ms. But blocks run in parallel. With 132 SMs and maybe 2 blocks per SM, 264 blocks run simultaneously. 1024 blocks / 264 = 3.9 waves.
